<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_technetium" title="Template:Infobox technetium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_technetium" title="Template talk:Infobox technetium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_technetium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Technetium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Tc</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 43. It is the lightest element whose isotopes are all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>; none are <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable</a>, excluding the fully ionized state of <sup>97</sup>Tc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takahashi_et_al_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takahashi_et_al-4">[4]</a></sup> Nearly all technetium is produced synthetically, and only about 18000 tons can be found at any given time in the Earth's crust. Naturally occurring technetium is a spontaneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission product</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> ore, the most common source, or the product of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> ores. This silvery gray, crystalline <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> lies between <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_7_element" title="Group 7 element">group 7</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, and its chemical properties are intermediate between those of these two adjacent elements. The most common naturally occurring isotope is <sup>99</sup>Tc.
</p><p>Many of technetium's properties were predicted by <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> before the element was discovered. Mendeleev noted a gap in his periodic table and gave the undiscovered element the provisional name <i><a href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements" title="Mendeleev's predicted elements">ekamanganese</a></i> (<i>Em</i>). In 1937, technetium (specifically the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technetium-97" title="Technetium-97">technetium-97</a> isotope) became the first predominantly artificial element to be produced, hence its name (from the Greek <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">τεχνητός</span>, meaning "synthetic or artificial", + <span class="nowrap"><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-ium#Suffix" title="wikt:-ium">-ium</a></i>).</span>
</p><p>One short-lived <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>-emitting <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a> of technetium—<a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">technetium-99m</a>—is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> for a wide variety of diagnostic tests, such as bone cancer diagnoses. The ground state of this <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>, technetium-99, is used as a gamma-ray-free source of <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particles</a>. Long-lived <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_technetium" title="Isotopes of technetium">technetium isotopes</a> produced commercially are by-products of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> and are extracted from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel rods</a>. Because no isotope of technetium has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> longer than 4.2 million years (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technetium-98" title="Technetium-98">technetium-98</a>), the 1952 detection of technetium in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>, helped to prove that stars can produce heavier elements.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_element_43">Search for element 43</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Search for element 43">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 1860s through 1871, early forms of the periodic table proposed by Dmitri Mendeleev contained a gap between <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> (element 42) and <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> (element 44). In 1871, Mendeleev predicted this missing element would occupy the empty place below <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> and have similar chemical properties. Mendeleev gave it the provisional name <i>ekamanganese</i> (from <i>eka</i>-, the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word for <i>one)</i> because the predicted element was one place down from the known element manganese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_misidentifications">Early misidentifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early misidentifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many early researchers, both before and after the periodic table was published, were eager to be the first to discover and name the missing element. Its location in the table suggested that it should be easier to find than other undiscovered elements.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irreproducible_results">Irreproducible results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Irreproducible results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_(1904-1945,_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="5184" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/220px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/330px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/440px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_(1904-1945,_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Periodisches System der Elemente (1904–1945, now at the <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk_University_of_Technology" title="Gdańsk University of Technology">Gdańsk University of Technology</a>): lack of elements: 84 polonium Po (though discovered as early as in 1898 by <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Maria Sklodowska-Curie</a>), 85 astatine At (1940, in Berkeley), 87 francium Fr (1939, in France), 93 neptunium Np (1940, in Berkeley) and other actinides and lanthanides. Old symbols for: 18 argon Ar (here: A), 43 technetium Tc (Ma, masurium, 1925, dismissed as an error and finally confirmed in 1937, Palermo), 54 xenon Xe (X), 86 radon, Rn (Em, emanation)</div></div></div>
<p>German chemists <a href="/wiki/Walter_Noddack" title="Walter Noddack">Walter Noddack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Berg_(scientist)" title="Otto Berg (scientist)">Otto Berg</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ida_Tacke" title="Ida Tacke">Ida Tacke</a> reported the discovery of element 75 and element 43 in 1925, and named element 43 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masurium" title="Masurium">masurium</a></i> (after <a href="/wiki/Masuria" title="Masuria">Masuria</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the region where Walter Noddack's family originated).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multidict_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-multidict-8">[8]</a></sup> The group bombarded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrocolumbite" title="Ferrocolumbite">columbite</a> with a beam of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and deduced element 43 was present by examining <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> diffraction <a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">spectrograms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p423_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p423-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the X-rays produced is related to the atomic number by a formula derived by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> in 1913. The team claimed to detect a faint X-ray signal at a wavelength produced by element 43. Later experimenters could not replicate the discovery, and it was dismissed as an error for many years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Still, in 1933, a series of articles on the discovery of elements quoted the name <i>masurium</i> for element 43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[note 1]</a></sup> Whether the 1925 team actually did discover element 43 is still debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_discovery_and_later_history">Official discovery and later history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Official discovery and later history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovery</a> of element 43 was finally confirmed in a 1937 experiment at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Palermo" title="University of Palermo">University of Palermo</a> in Sicily by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Perrier" title="Carlo Perrier">Carlo Perrier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Segr%C3%A8" title="Emilio Segrè">Emilio Segrè</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p164_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p164-15">[14]</a></sup> In mid-1936, Segrè visited the United States, first <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> in New York and then the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Berkeley_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory">Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory</a> in California. He persuaded <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> inventor <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Lawrence" title="Ernest Lawrence">Ernest Lawrence</a> to let him take back some discarded cyclotron parts that had become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>. Lawrence mailed him a <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> foil that had been part of the deflector in the cyclotron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Segrè enlisted his colleague Perrier to attempt to prove, through comparative chemistry, that the molybdenum activity was indeed from an element with the atomic number 43. In 1937 they succeeded in isolating the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technetium-95" title="Technetium-95">technetium-95m</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technetium-97" title="Technetium-97">technetium-97</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-segre_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-segre-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blocks_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">[17]</a></sup> University of Palermo officials wanted them to name their discovery "<i>panormium</i>", after the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name for <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, <i>Panormus</i>. In 1947<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-segre_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-segre-17">[16]</a></sup> element 43 was named after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word <i>τεχνητός</i>, meaning "artificial", since it was the first element to be artificially produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history-origin_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-origin-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multidict_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-multidict-8">[8]</a></sup> Segrè returned to Berkeley and met <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>. They isolated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable_isotope" title="Metastable isotope">metastable isotope</a> <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">technetium-99m</a>, which is now used in some ten million medical diagnostic procedures annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1952, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Paul_W._Merrill" title="Paul W. Merrill">Paul W. Merrill</a> in California detected the <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">spectral signature</a> of technetium (specifically <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of 403.1 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>, 423.8 nm, 426.2 nm, and 429.7 nm) in light from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification#Class_S" title="Stellar classification">S-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> The stars were near the end of their lives, yet were rich in this short-lived element, indicating that it was being produced in the stars by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a>. This evidence bolstered the hypothesis that heavier elements are the product of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> in stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blocks_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">[17]</a></sup> More recently, such observations provided evidence that elements are formed by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s8_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since that discovery, there have been many searches in terrestrial materials for natural sources of technetium. In 1962, technetium-99 was isolated and identified in <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">pitchblende</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> in extremely small quantities (about 0.2 ng/kg);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s8_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">[20]</a></sup> there it originates as a <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> product of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactor</a> contains evidence that significant amounts of technetium-99 were produced and have since decayed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruthenium-99" title="Ruthenium-99">ruthenium-99</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s8_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium is a silvery-gray radioactive <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> with an appearance similar to <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, commonly obtained as a gray powder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">[21]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a> of the pure metal is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close-packed" title="Close-packed">close-packed</a>. Atomic technetium has characteristic <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission lines</a> at these <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of light: 363.3 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>, 403.1 nm, 426.2 nm, 429.7 nm, and 485.3 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The metal form is slightly <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a>, meaning its <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">magnetic dipoles</a> align with external <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, but will assume random orientations once the field is removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">[23]</a></sup> Pure, metallic, single-crystal technetium becomes a <a href="/wiki/Type-II_superconductor" title="Type-II superconductor">type-II superconductor</a> at temperatures below 7.46 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[24]</a></sup> Below this temperature, technetium has a very high <a href="/wiki/London_penetration_depth" title="London penetration depth">magnetic penetration depth</a>, greater than any other element except <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium is located in the <a href="/wiki/Group_7_element" title="Group 7 element">seventh group</a> of the periodic table, between <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>. As predicted by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_periodic_table" title="History of the periodic table">periodic law</a>, its chemical properties are between those two elements. Of the two, technetium more closely resembles rhenium, particularly in its chemical inertness and tendency to form <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Unlike manganese, technetium does not readily form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cations</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> with a net positive charge). Technetium exhibits nine <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> from −1 to +7, with +4, +5, and +7 being the most common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">[27]</a></sup> Technetium dissolves in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, but it is not soluble in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> of any concentration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metallic technetium slowly <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> in moist air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">[27]</a></sup> and, in powder form, burns in <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.
</p><p>Technetium can catalyse the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and this property is due to its multiplicity of valencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> This caused a problem in the separation of plutonium from uranium in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">nuclear fuel processing</a>, where hydrazine is used as a protective reductant to keep plutonium in the trivalent rather than the more stable tetravalent state. The problem was exacerbated by the mutually-enhanced solvent extraction of technetium and zirconium at the previous stage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> and required a process modification.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pertechnetate_and_derivatives">Pertechnetate and derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pertechnetate and derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pertechnetate1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="96" data-file-width="125" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/200px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/300px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/400px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pertechnetate1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pertechnetate is one of the most available forms of technetium.  It is structurally related to <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The most prevalent form of technetium that is easily accessible is sodium pertechnetate, Na[TcO<sub>4</sub>].  The majority of this material is produced by radioactive decay from [<sup>99</sup>MoO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuclmed_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-nuclmed-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>[<sup>99</sup>MoO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>  →   [<sup>99</sup>TcO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup>  +  γ</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">Pertechnetate</a> (tetroxidotechnetate) <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> behaves analogously to perchlorate, both of which are <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), it is only a weak oxidizing agent.
</p><p>Related to pertechnetate is <a href="/wiki/Technetium(VII)_oxide" title="Technetium(VII) oxide">heptoxide</a>.  This pale-yellow, volatile solid is produced by oxidation of Tc metal and related precursors:
</p>
<dl><dd>4 Tc + 7 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></dd></dl>
<p>It is a very rare example of a molecular metal oxide, other examples being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OsO4" title="OsO4">OsO<sub>4</sub></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RuO4" title="RuO4">RuO<sub>4</sub></a>.  It adopts a <a href="/wiki/Centrosymmetry" title="Centrosymmetry">centrosymmetric</a> structure with two types of Tc−O bonds with 167 and 184 pm bond lengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium heptoxide hydrolyzes to <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetic_acid" title="Pertechnetic acid">pertechnetic acid</a>, depending on the pH:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + 2 OH<sup>−</sup> →  2 TcO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O  →  2 HTcO<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>HTcO<sub>4</sub> is a strong acid. In concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, [TcO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> converts to the octahedral form TcO<sub>3</sub>(OH)(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>, the conjugate base of the hypothetical tri<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquo_complex" title="Aquo complex">aquo complex</a> [TcO<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_chalcogenide_derivatives">Other chalcogenide derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other chalcogenide derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium forms a dioxide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_dichalcogenide" title="Metal dichalcogenide">disulfide</a>, di<a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a>, and di<a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">telluride</a>.  An ill-defined Tc<sub>2</sub>S<sub>7</sub> forms upon treating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pertechnate" title="Pertechnate">pertechnate</a> with hydrogen sulfide. It thermally decomposes into disulfide and elemental sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>  Similarly the dioxide can be produced by reduction of the Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.
</p><p>Unlike the case for rhenium, a trioxide has not been isolated for technetium. However, TcO<sub>3</sub> has been identified in the gas phase using <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_hydride_and_halide_complexes">Simple hydride and halide complexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Simple hydride and halide complexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium forms the simple complex <span class="chemf nowrap">TcH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span>.  The potassium salt is <a href="/wiki/Isostructural" title="Isostructural">isostructural</a> with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nonahydridorhenate" title="Potassium nonahydridorhenate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ReH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following binary (containing only two elements) technetium halides are known: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TcF6" title="TcF6">TcF<sub>6</sub></a>, TcF<sub>5</sub>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TcCl4" title="TcCl4">TcCl<sub>4</sub></a>, TcBr<sub>4</sub>, TcBr<sub>3</sub>, α-TcCl<sub>3</sub>, β-TcCl<sub>3</sub>, TcI<sub>3</sub>, α-TcCl<sub>2</sub>, and β-TcCl<sub>2</sub>.  The <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> range from Tc(VI) to Tc(II). Technetium halides exhibit different structure types, such as molecular octahedral complexes, extended chains, layered sheets, and metal clusters arranged in a three-dimensional network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">[41]</a></sup> These compounds are produced by combining the metal and halogen or by less direct reactions.
</p><p>TcCl<sub>4</sub> is obtained by chlorination of Tc metal or Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> Upon heating, TcCl<sub>4</sub> gives the corresponding Tc(III) and Tc(II) chlorides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>TcCl<sub>4</sub> → α-TcCl<sub>3</sub> + 1/2 Cl<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>TcCl<sub>3</sub> → β-TcCl<sub>2</sub> + 1/2 Cl<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="791" data-file-width="1100" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/180px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/270px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/360px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TcCl<sub>4</sub> forms chain-like structures, similar to the behavior of several other metal tetrachlorides.</div></div></div>
<p>The structure of TcCl<sub>4</sub> is composed of infinite zigzag chains of edge-sharing TcCl<sub>6</sub> octahedra.  It is isomorphous to transition metal tetrachlorides of <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two polymorphs of technetium trichloride exist, α- and β-TcCl<sub>3</sub>. The α polymorph is also denoted as Tc<sub>3</sub>Cl<sub>9</sub>. It adopts a confacial <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry#Bioctahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">bioctahedral structure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>  It is prepared by treating the chloro-acetate Tc<sub>2</sub>(O<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub> with HCl. Like <a href="/wiki/Trirhenium_nonachloride" title="Trirhenium nonachloride">Re<sub>3</sub>Cl<sub>9</sub></a>, the structure of the α-polymorph consists of triangles with short M-M distances. β-TcCl<sub>3</sub> features octahedral Tc centers, which are organized in pairs, as seen also for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molybdenum_trichloride" title="Molybdenum trichloride">molybdenum trichloride</a>. TcBr<sub>3</sub> does not adopt the structure of either trichloride phase.  Instead it has the structure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molybdenum_tribromide" title="Molybdenum tribromide">molybdenum tribromide</a>, consisting of chains of confacial octahedra with alternating short and long Tc—Tc contacts.  TcI<sub>3</sub> has the same structure as the high temperature phase of <a href="/wiki/Titanium(III)_iodide" title="Titanium(III) iodide">TiI<sub>3</sub></a>, featuring chains of confacial octahedra with equal Tc—Tc contacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several anionic technetium halides are known.  The binary tetrahalides can be converted to the hexahalides [TcX<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> (X = F, Cl, Br, I), which adopt <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral molecular geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s8_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">[20]</a></sup>  More reduced halides form anionic clusters with Tc–Tc bonds.  The situation is similar for the related elements of Mo, W, Re.  These clusters have the nuclearity Tc<sub>4</sub>, Tc<sub>6</sub>, Tc<sub>8</sub>, and Tc<sub>13</sub>. The more stable Tc<sub>6</sub> and Tc<sub>8</sub> clusters have prism shapes where vertical pairs of Tc atoms are connected by triple bonds and the planar atoms by single bonds. Every technetium atom makes six bonds, and the remaining valence electrons can be saturated by one axial and two <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligand</a> halogen atoms such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_and_organometallic_complexes">Coordination and organometallic complexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coordination and organometallic complexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tc_CNCH2CMe2(OMe)_6Cation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="961" data-file-width="1018" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/220px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/330px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/440px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tc_CNCH2CMe2(OMe)_6Cation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Technetium_(99mTc)_sestamibi" title="Technetium (99mTc) sestamibi">Technetium (99mTc) sestamibiis</a> ("Cardiolite") widely used for imaging of the heart.</div></div></div>
<p>Technetium forms a variety of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> with organic ligands.  Many have been well-investigated because of their relevance to nuclear medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium forms a variety of compounds with Tc–C bonds, i.e. organotechnetium complexes.  Prominent members of this class are complexes with CO, arene, and cyclopentadienyl ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">[45]</a></sup>  The binary carbonyl Tc<sub>2</sub>(CO)<sub>10</sub> is a white volatile solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> In this molecule, two technetium atoms are bound to each other; each atom is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedra</a> of five carbonyl ligands. The bond length between technetium atoms, 303 pm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> is significantly larger than the distance between two atoms in metallic technetium (272 pm). Similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">carbonyls</a> are formed by technetium's <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congeners</a>, manganese and rhenium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>  Interest in organotechnetium compounds has also been motivated by applications in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">[45]</a></sup> Unusual for other metal carbonyls, Tc forms aquo-carbonyl complexes, prominent being [Tc(CO)<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_technetium" title="Isotopes of technetium">Isotopes of technetium</a></div>
<p>Technetium, with <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (denoted <i>Z</i>) 43, is the lowest-numbered element in the periodic table of which all isotopes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>. The second-lightest exclusively radioactive element, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, has an atomic number of 61.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">Atomic nuclei</a> with an odd number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> are less stable than those with even numbers, even when the total number of <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> (protons + <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>) is even,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> and odd numbered elements have fewer stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.
</p><p>The most stable <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioactive isotopes</a> are technetium-98 with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 4.2 million years (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">Ma</a>), technetium-97 with 2.6 Ma, and technetium-99 with 211,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">[51]</a></sup> Thirty other radioisotopes have been characterized with <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass numbers</a> ranging from 85 to 118.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">[51]</a></sup> Most of these have half-lives that are less than an hour, the exceptions being technetium-93 (half-life: 2.73 hours), technetium-94 (half-life: 4.88 hours), technetium-95 (half-life: 20 hours), and technetium-96 (half-life: 4.3 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> for isotopes lighter than technetium-98 (<sup>98</sup>Tc) is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> (<i>Z</i> = 42).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">[51]</a></sup> For technetium-98 and heavier isotopes, the primary mode is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta emission</a> (the emission of an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>), producing <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> (<i>Z</i> = 44), with the exception that technetium-100 can decay both by beta emission and electron capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium also has numerous <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>, which are isotopes with one or more <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited</a> nucleons. Technetium-97m (<sup>97m</sup>Tc; 'm' stands for <a href="/wiki/Metastability" title="Metastability">metastability</a>) is the most stable, with a half-life of 91 days (0.0965 MeV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">[52]</a></sup> This is followed by technetium-95m (half-life: 61 days, 0.03 MeV), and technetium-99m (half-life: 6.01 hours, 0.142 MeV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">[52]</a></sup> Technetium-99m emits only <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> and decays to technetium-99.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium-99 (<sup>99</sup>Tc) is a major product of the fission of uranium-235 (<sup>235</sup>U), making it the most common and most readily available isotope of technetium. One gram of technetium-99 produces 6.2×10<sup>8</sup> disintegrations a second (that is, 0.62 G<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a>/g).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Technetium occurs naturally in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> in minute concentrations of about 0.003 parts per trillion. This totals about 18000 tonnes at any given time, assuming the mass of the Earth's crust is <span style="display:none;">6000000000000000000000</span>6×10<sup>21</sup> kilograms. Technetium is so rare because technetium-98's <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> is only 4.2 million years. More than a thousand of such periods have passed since the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, so the probability for the survival of even one atom of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> technetium is effectively zero. However, small amounts exist as spontaneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ores</a>. A kilogram of uranium contains an estimated 1 nanogram (10<sup>−9</sup> g) of technetium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blocks_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars with the spectral types S-, M-, and N contain a spectral absorption line indicating the presence of technetium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> These red-giants are known informally as <a href="/wiki/Technetium_star" title="Technetium star">technetium stars</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_waste_product">Fission waste product</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fission waste product">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to the rare natural occurrence, bulk quantities of technetium-99 are produced each year from <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel rods</a>, which contain various fission products. The fission of a gram of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> yields 27 mg of technetium-99, giving technetium a <a href="/wiki/Fission_product_yield" title="Fission product yield">fission product yield</a> of 6.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">[23]</a></sup> Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> isotopes produce similar yields of technetium, such as 4.9% from <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">uranium-233</a> and 6.21% from <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup>  An estimated 49,000 T<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a> (78 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">metric tons</a>) of technetium was produced in nuclear reactors between 1983 and 1994, by far the dominant source of terrestrial technetium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leon_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-leon-61">[59]</a></sup> Only a fraction of the production is used commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[note 3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium-99 is produced by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> of both uranium-235 and plutonium-239. It is therefore present in <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">fission bomb</a> explosions. Its decay, measured in <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">becquerels</a> per amount of spent fuel, is the dominant contributor to nuclear waste radioactivity after about 10<sup>4</sup> to 10<sup>6</sup> years after the creation of the nuclear waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">[58]</a></sup> From 1945 to 1994, an estimated 160 T<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a> (about 250 kg) of technetium-99 was released into the environment during atmospheric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_test" title="Nuclear test">nuclear tests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> The amount of technetium-99 from nuclear reactors released into the environment up to 1986 is on the order of 1000 TBq (about 1600 kg), primarily by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_reprocessing" title="Nuclear fuel reprocessing">nuclear fuel reprocessing</a>; most of this was discharged into the sea. Reprocessing methods have reduced emissions since then, but as of 2005 the primary release of technetium-99 into the environment is by the <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Sellafield</a> plant, which released an estimated 550 TBq (about 900 kg) from 1995–1999 into the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leon_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-leon-61">[59]</a></sup> From 2000 onwards the amount has been limited by regulation to 90 TBq (about 140 kg) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup>  Discharge of technetium into the sea resulted in contamination of some seafood with minuscule quantities of this element. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_lobster" title="European lobster">European lobster</a> and fish from west <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> contain about 1 Bq/kg of technetium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_product_for_commercial_use">Fission product for commercial use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fission product for commercial use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Metastability" title="Metastability">metastable</a> isotope technetium-99m is continuously produced as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission product</a> from the fission of uranium or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{238}_{92}U -&gt;[{\ce {sf}}] ^{137}_{53}I + ^{99}_{39}Y + 2^{1}_{0}n}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{238}_{92}U -&gt;[{\ce {sf}}] ^{137}_{53}I + ^{99}_{39}Y + 2^{1}_{0}n}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/69e432873016029d0ca91cbd92c76fc15631fd66" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; margin-top: -0.293ex; width:24.636ex; height:4.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {^{99}_{39}Y -&gt;[\beta^-][1.47\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{40}Zr -&gt;[\beta^-][2.1\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{41}Nb -&gt;[\beta^-][15.0\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{42}Mo -&gt;[\beta^-][65.94\,{\ce {h}}] ^{99}_{43}Tc -&gt;[\beta^-][211,100\,{\ce {y}}] ^{99}_{44}Ru}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{99}_{39}Y -&gt;[\beta^-][1.47\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{40}Zr -&gt;[\beta^-][2.1\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{41}Nb -&gt;[\beta^-][15.0\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{42}Mo -&gt;[\beta^-][65.94\,{\ce {h}}] ^{99}_{43}Tc -&gt;[\beta^-][211,100\,{\ce {y}}] ^{99}_{44}Ru}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33a9d13cb741fa5c8efce11540847d7deac9654b" style="vertical-align: -2.079ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.592ex; width:62.84ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Because used fuel is allowed to stand for several years before reprocessing, all molybdenum-99 and technetium-99m is decayed by the time that the fission products are separated from the major <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> in conventional <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">nuclear reprocessing</a>. The liquid left after plutonium–uranium extraction (<a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a>) contains a high concentration of technetium as <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> but almost all of this is technetium-99, not technetium-99m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The vast majority of the technetium-99m used in medical work is produced by irradiating dedicated <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium#Highly_enriched_uranium_(HEU)" title="Enriched uranium">highly enriched uranium</a> targets in a reactor, extracting molybdenum-99 from the targets in reprocessing facilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuclmed_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-nuclmed-33">[31]</a></sup> and recovering at the diagnostic center the technetium-99m produced upon decay of molybdenum-99.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAS_Report_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAS_Report-70">[66]</a></sup> Molybdenum-99 in the form of molybdate <span class="chemf nowrap">MoO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorbed</a> onto acid alumina (<span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_shielding" title="Radiation shielding">shielded</a> <a href="/wiki/Column_chromatography" title="Column chromatography">column chromatograph</a> inside a <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m_generator" title="Technetium-99m generator">technetium-99m generator</a> ("technetium cow", also occasionally called a "molybdenum cow"). Molybdenum-99 has a half-life of 67 hours, so short-lived technetium-99m (half-life: 6 hours), which results from its decay, is being constantly produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blocks_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">[17]</a></sup> The soluble <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> can then be chemically extracted by <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saline_solution" title="Saline solution">saline solution</a>. A drawback of this process is that it requires targets containing uranium-235, which are subject to the security precautions of fissile materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1788" data-file-width="1392" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/220px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/330px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/440px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first technetium-99m generator, unshielded, 1958. A Tc-99m <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> solution is being eluted from Mo-99 <a href="/wiki/Molybdate" title="Molybdate">molybdate</a> bound to a chromatographic substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Almost two-thirds of the world's supply comes from two reactors; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Research_Universal_Reactor" title="National Research Universal Reactor">National Research Universal Reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Chalk_River_Laboratories" title="Chalk River Laboratories">Chalk River Laboratories</a>  in Ontario, Canada, and the <a href="/wiki/Petten_nuclear_reactor" title="Petten nuclear reactor">High Flux Reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Research_and_Consultancy_Group" title="Nuclear Research and Consultancy Group">Nuclear Research and Consultancy Group</a> in Petten, Netherlands. All major reactors that produce technetium-99m were built in the 1960s and are close to the <a href="/wiki/End-of-life_(product)" title="End-of-life (product)">end of life</a>. The two new Canadian <a href="/wiki/Multipurpose_Applied_Physics_Lattice_Experiment" title="Multipurpose Applied Physics Lattice Experiment">Multipurpose Applied Physics Lattice Experiment</a> reactors planned and built to produce 200% of the demand of technetium-99m relieved all other producers from building their own reactors. With the cancellation of the already tested reactors in 2008, the future supply of technetium-99m became problematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chalk River reactor was shut down for maintenance in August 2009, and reopened in August 2010. The Petten reactor had a 6-month scheduled maintenance shutdown on Friday, February 19, 2010, and reopened September 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup> With millions of procedures relying on technetium-99m every year, the low supply has left a gap, leaving some practitioners to revert to techniques not used for 20 years. Somewhat allaying this issue is an announcement from the Polish <a href="/wiki/Maria_reactor" title="Maria reactor">Maria research reactor</a> that they have developed a technique to isolate technetium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NY_Times_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NY_Times-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Waste disposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The long half-life of technetium-99 and its potential to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anionic" title="Anionic">anionic</a> species creates a major concern for long-term <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">disposal of radioactive waste</a>. Many of the processes designed to remove fission products in reprocessing plants aim at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cationic" title="Cationic">cationic</a> species such as <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a>). Hence the pertechnetate escapes through those processes. Current disposal options favor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_repository" title="Geological repository">burial</a> in continental, geologically stable rock. The primary danger with such practice is the likelihood that the waste will contact water, which could leach radioactive contamination into the environment. The anionic pertechnetate and <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> tend not to adsorb into the surfaces of minerals, and are likely to be washed away. By comparison <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>  tend to bind to soil particles. Technetium could be immobilized by some environments, such as microbial activity in lake bottom sediments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> and the <b><a href="/wiki/Environmental_chemistry" title="Environmental chemistry">environmental chemistry</a></b> of technetium is an area of active research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative disposal method, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a>, has been demonstrated at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> for technetium-99. In this process, the technetium (technetium-99 as a metal target) is bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> to form the short-lived technetium-100 (half-life = 16 seconds) which decays by beta decay to <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>-100. If recovery of usable ruthenium is a goal, an extremely pure technetium target is needed; if small traces of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinide" title="Minor actinide">minor actinides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> are present in the target, they are likely to undergo fission and form more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission products</a> which increase the radioactivity of the irradiated target. The formation of ruthenium-106 (half-life 374 days) from the 'fresh fission' is likely to increase the activity of the final ruthenium metal, which will then require a longer cooling time after irradiation before the ruthenium can be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The actual separation of technetium-99 from spent nuclear fuel is a long process. During <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">fuel reprocessing</a>, it comes out as a component of the highly radioactive waste liquid. After sitting for several years, the radioactivity reduces to a level where extraction of the long-lived isotopes, including technetium-99, becomes feasible. A series of chemical processes yields technetium-99 metal of high purity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutron_activation">Neutron activation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neutron activation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molybdenum-99" title="Molybdenum-99">Molybdenum-99</a>, which decays to form technetium-99m, can be formed by the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of molybdenum-98.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_TECDOC-1340_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_TECDOC-1340-80">[76]</a></sup> When needed, other technetium isotopes are not produced in significant quantities by fission, but are manufactured by neutron irradiation of parent isotopes (for example, technetium-97 can be made by neutron irradiation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruthenium-96" title="Ruthenium-96">ruthenium-96</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particle_accelerators">Particle accelerators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Particle accelerators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The feasibility of technetium-99m production with the 22-MeV-proton bombardment of a molybdenum-100 target in medical cyclotrons following the reaction <sup>100</sup>Mo(p,2n)<sup>99m</sup>Tc was demonstrated in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> The recent shortages of medical technetium-99m reignited the interest in its production by proton bombardment of isotopically-enriched (&gt;99.5%) molybdenum-100 targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-20150530_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20150530-83">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup> Other techniques are being investigated for obtaining molybdenum-99 from molybdenum-100 via (n,2n) or (γ,n) reactions in particle accelerators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_medicine_and_biology">Nuclear medicine and biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nuclear medicine and biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">Technetium-99m</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:214px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg"><img alt="Upper image: two drop-like features merged at their bottoms; they have a yellow centre and a red rim on a black background. Caption: Graves' Disease Tc-Uptake 16%. Lower image: red dots on black background. Caption: 250 Gy (30mCi) + Prednison." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="212" height="480" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg" width="212"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Technetium <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">scintigraphy</a> of a neck of <a href="/wiki/Graves%27_disease" title="Graves' disease">Graves' disease</a> patient</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">Technetium-99m</a> ("m" indicates that this is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer#Metastable_isomers" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable</a> nuclear isomer) is used in radioactive isotope <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">medical tests</a>. For example, Technetium-99m is a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> that medical imaging equipment tracks in the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blocks_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-20150530_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20150530-83">[79]</a></sup> It is well suited to the role because it emits readily detectable 140 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, and its half-life is 6.01 hours (meaning that about 94% of it decays to technetium-99 in 24 hours).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">[23]</a></sup> The chemistry of technetium allows it to be bound to a variety of biochemical compounds, each of which determines how it is metabolized and deposited in the body, and this single isotope can be used for a multitude of diagnostic tests. More than 50 common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiopharmaceuticals" title="Radiopharmaceuticals">radiopharmaceuticals</a> are based on technetium-99m for imaging and functional studies of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, heart muscle, <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gall_bladder" title="Gall bladder">gall bladder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">skeleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The longer-lived isotope, technetium-95m with a half-life of 61 days, is used as a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> to study the movement of technetium in the environment and in plant and animal systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_and_chemical">Industrial and chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Industrial and chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium-99 decays almost entirely by beta decay, emitting beta particles with consistent low energies and no accompanying gamma rays. Moreover, its long half-life means that this emission decreases very slowly with time. It can also be extracted to a high chemical and isotopic purity from radioactive waste. For these reasons, it is a <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST) standard beta emitter, and is used for equipment calibration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> Technetium-99 has also been proposed for optoelectronic devices and <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanoscale</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_battery" title="Nuclear battery">nuclear batteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, technetium can serve as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. In processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Dehydrogenation" title="Dehydrogenation">dehydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isopropyl_alcohol" title="Isopropyl alcohol">isopropyl alcohol</a>, it is a far more effective catalyst than either rhenium or palladium. However, its radioactivity is a major problem in safe catalytic applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>When steel is immersed in water, adding a small concentration (55 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_notation" title="Parts per notation">ppm</a>) of potassium pertechnetate(VII) to the water protects the <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> from corrosion, even if the temperature is raised to 250 °C (523 K).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corr_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-corr-93">[89]</a></sup> For this reason, pertechnetate has been used as an anodic <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> inhibitor for steel, although technetium's radioactivity poses problems that limit this application to self-contained systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup> While (for example) <span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> can also inhibit corrosion, it requires a concentration ten times as high. In one experiment, a specimen of carbon steel was kept in an aqueous solution of pertechnetate for 20 years and was still uncorroded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corr_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-corr-93">[89]</a></sup> The mechanism by which pertechnetate prevents corrosion is not well understood, but seems to involve the reversible formation of a thin surface layer (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). One theory holds that the pertechnetate reacts with the steel surface to form a layer of technetium <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">dioxide</a> which prevents further corrosion; the same effect explains how iron powder can be used to remove pertechnetate from water. (<a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">Activated carbon</a> can also be used for the same purpose.) The effect disappears rapidly if the concentration of pertechnetate falls below the minimum concentration or if too high a concentration of other ions is added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s91_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-s91-95">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>As noted, the radioactive nature of technetium (3 MBq/L at the concentrations required) makes this corrosion protection impractical in almost all situations. Nevertheless, corrosion protection by pertechnetate ions was proposed (but never adopted) for use in <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s91_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-s91-95">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Technetium plays no natural biological role and is not normally found in the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">[21]</a></sup> Technetium is produced in quantity by nuclear fission, and spreads more readily than many radionuclides. It appears to have low chemical toxicity. For example, no significant change in blood formula, body and organ weights, and food consumption could be detected for rats which ingested up to 15 µg of technetium-99 per gram of food for several weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> The radiological toxicity of technetium (per unit of mass) is a function of compound, type of radiation for the isotope in question, and the isotope's half-life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>All isotopes of technetium must be handled carefully. The most common isotope, technetium-99, is a weak beta emitter; such radiation is stopped by the walls of laboratory glassware.  The primary hazard when working with technetium is inhalation of dust; such <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> in the lungs can pose a significant cancer risk. For most work, careful handling in a <a href="/wiki/Fume_hood" title="Fume hood">fume hood</a> is sufficient, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glove_box" title="Glove box">glove box</a> is not needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
